<HR>
<b><a name = "1964">1964</a></b>

<li>
<!--a href = "icons/stamp.israeli.1964.jpg"><img src =
"icons/stamp.israeli.1964.wholetiny.jpg"
border = 0 ALIGN = "RIGHT"></a-->
<a href = "icons/stamp.israeli.1964.jpg"><img src =
"icons/stamp.israeli.1964.tiny.jpg"
border = 0 ALIGN = "RIGHT"></a>
The earliest Left handed DNA that we are aware of
is an Israeli stamp from 1964.
The image was sent to me by
<a href = "http://www.biochem.purdue.edu/~bmb/faculty/tess.html">Irwin Tessman</a>
(Department of Biological Sciences
Purdue University
West Lafayette IN 47907-1392).
<!--
He says
"The stamp came to my attention via the paper (Acc. Chem. Res. 1992,
25:24-31) by Zvi Rappoport on chemophilately, which shows the stamp in the
lower right corner of Figure 2, ... and is described
and highlighted on page 30."
Chemistry on stamps (chemophilately). Rappoport, Z. Acc. Chem. Res., 25(1), 24- 31, 1992.
-->
The stamp is in 
"Chemistry on stamps (chemophilately)".
<a href = "http://chem.ch.huji.ac.il/employee/rapport/irapport.htm">Rappoport, Z.</a>
Acc. Chem. Res., 25(1), 24- 31, 1992.
It is in the lower right corner of Figure 2, and is described
on page 30 as having 'the "abnormal" helicity of DNA'.
<a href = "icons/stamp.israeli.1964.jpg">Click to see the complete beautiful
stamp</a>.
<br><img src="icons/new.gif"> as of 1998 Nov 23

<HR>
<b><a name = "1978">1978</a></b>

<li>
The now out of print book,
<a href =
"http://www.amazon.com/exec/obidos/ASIN/0805396098/qid%3D924906861/002-4985817-8618603">Molecular
Biology of the Gene</a>
by James D. Watson
(W. A. Benjamin, Inc, Menlo Park, California,
third Edition,
ISBN 0-8053-9609-8)
has a series of left handed DNA pictures on pages
228 (fig 9-18),
263 (fig 10-10 and 10-11),
268 (fig 10-16),
290 (fig 11-8),
292 (fig 11-9),
and
419 (fig 15-9).
<font size = 3 color = #ff00ff><!--purple-->
(Thanks to
Richard Wolf (wolf@umbc.edu)
for pointing this one out!)
</font>
<img src="icons/new.gif"> as of 1999 April 23.
"There is at least one additional drawing of left-handed DNA
on p. 216 (fig 9-9).
Also, the Fig. 11-8 on p. 290 is particularly cute in presenting a
right-handed structure amidst two left-handed flanks in the same drawing
(the central portion showing a hybrid duplex of RNA/DNA, though)."
<font size = 3 color = #ff00ff><!--purple-->
(Thanks to
Richard Egel, richard.egel@molbio.ku.dk
for pointing this one out!)
</font>
<img src="icons/new.gif"> as of 2000 August 18.

<HR>
<b><a name = "1980">around 1980</a></b>

<li> Until Dr. Tessman informed me otherwise in November 1998,
the earliest example I was aware of is a beautiful
drawing of a circular plasmid by the <b>Beckman Company</b>
published around 1980.  "Our experience covers
over 40 years of innovation and unmatched quality".

<li>
On the cover of
Nature Volume 284 Number 5757,
17 April 1980 is on left handed "Selfish DNA"
(a series of left-handed twisting snakes eating each other in
a circle).
The person who found it writes:
<blockquote>
<p>
Hi there!  Yes, I am a Newsweek reader.  I am also an artist responsible for
perhaps one of the first left-handed boo-boos.
</p><p>
My husband and his old advisor (Doolittle and Sapienza) wrote a piece on
"Selfish DNA" that was accepted by Nature.  Their paper and another by Crick
and Orgel became the magazine's cover topic.  Lucky me!  I got to draw for
Nature!
</p><p>
No one noticed that my design was left-handed until the journal was in press.
I will admit that the scientists thought it was pretty funny (well, what
could they do at that point?).
</p><p>
In the US the background color was an ugly light orange, but the British
version was a vivid red.
<!--
I have copies of the red cover if you would like
one (however, I don't own the copyright, and so I'd assume you'd have to ask
Nature about that).
-->
</p><p>
<!--
It is Nature Volume 284 Number 5757
        17 April 1980  "Selfish DNA"
-->
</p><p>
I'm starting to feel like a real part of history here...uh, infamous too.
</p><p>
<!--
My name is Linda Angeloff Sapienza and my email address is LASplumas@aol.com
-->
...
</p><p>
Thanks for a great site!
</p>
</blockquote>
<font size = 3 color = #ff00ff><!--purple-->
(Thanks to
Linda Angeloff Sapienza, LASplumas@aol.com,
for pointing this one out!)
</font>
<br><img src="icons/new.gif"> as of 2000 May 30

<HR>
<b><a name = "1982">1982</a></b>

<li>
<b>Principles of Biochemistry</b> by Albert L. Lehninger,
Worth Publishers, Inc., New York.
ISBN 0-87901-136-X.
(added 1998 April 8)
Page 850 shows a relaxed left handed DNA circle which when acted
on by DNA gyrase with the hydrolysis of ATP to ADP + Pi becomes a
supertwisted right-handed DNA.

<HR>
<b><a name = "1983">1983</a></b>

<a href =
"http://www.amazon.com/exec/obidos/ISBN=0062730991">
<img src = "icons/cartoon.guide.revised.gif"
border = 0 ALIGN = "RIGHT"></a>
<li>
<b>The Cartoon Guide To Genetics</b>
by
<b> Larry Gonick and Mark Wheelis </b> (Barnes & Noble, NY, 1983),
is a wonderful and funny introduction but unfortunately the
cute RNA polymerase on the cover is chewing away at a left
handed DNA.
The cover of the
<a href =
"http://www.amazon.com/exec/obidos/ISBN=0062730991">updated
edition</a> has the even odder DNA that is changing twist as the RNA polymerase
moves by.
(It's a great book anyway!!)<br clear = all>

<li>
In a letter
<b>Nature</b> 305:176 (1983)
John H. Wilson and Peter B. Berget point out that
in the advertisement for the
Symposium: Molecular Biology Now & Tomorrow was a DNA that was
simultaneously left and right handed!
<font size = 3 color = #ff00ff><!--purple-->
(Thanks to
<a href="mailto:jwilson@bcm.tmc.edu">John Wilson</a>
for pointing this one out.)
</font>

<HR>
<b><a name = "1989">1989</a></b>

<li> <b>Arthur Kornberg</b>, discoverer of DNA polymerase,
wrote a book:
<blockquote>
For the love of enzymes: the odyssey of a biochemist
Cambridge, Mass., Harvard University Press, 1989.
</blockquote>
In the section on "Astonishing Machines of Replication"
on page 227, figure 7-14
there is a figleaf covering the point of DNA replication,
all of the strands coming out of it are left handed.

<a name = "space.invaders"></a>
<li> An <b>Amgen</b> advertisement in Nature 340, 17 August 1989,
shows a replicating left-handed DNA in space over the North American
continent, with the words "Amgen Biologicals: bringing world class
products to the forefront of biomedical research".
Space invaders?

<li> The <b>Trends in Biotechnology</b> cover
January 1989, Volume 7 No.1 shows
both left and right-hand forms coming from a single plant.

<p>
<font size = -1>
version = 2.00 of leftyear1995.html 2002 March 4
</font>
</p>

<HR>
<b><a name = "1990">1990</a></b>

<li> The <b>Trends in Biotechnology</b> cover
June 1990, Volume 8 No.6 shows
an SEM image of a left-handed DNA with the words
"Scanning Tunnelling Microscopy of Biomolecules".
In the article itself ("Scanning tunnelling
Microscopy in Biotechnology", P. G. Arscott and V. A. Bloomfield,
page 153), fig 2 shows a left-handed DNA with a figure legend
stating that it is "in the left-handed Z form".
Yet on page iii is "Front Cover ... A single molecule of B-form DNA ..."

<li> The <b>IBI 1990 Catalog</b> cover
two kinds of model, both left handed.

<li> <b>Perkin Elmer Spring 1990</b> Biotechnology Catalog cover

<li> <b>Bio-Rad</b> Bio-Radiations No. 76, 1990, page 4, "DNA
Preparation for Forensic Analysis" with a left-handed helix.
To track the space invaders?  "Image: Nelson L. Max".

<li> A <b>Genosys</b> advertisement in
Trends in Biochemical Research 15(4) April 1990. 
"Some good things you have to wait for." (under a picture of wine and glasses)
"Others you don't." (under a picture of left-handed DNA).

<li> "Logic of the <i>Escherichia coli</i> cell cycle",
Richard D'Ari and Philippe Bouloc,
cartoon in
<b>TIBS</b> May 1990 15(5) 191-194.

<li> <b>Clontech</b> advertisement in
Nucleic Acids Research 18(4) July 25, 1990. 
"we can offer you guaranteed quality and excellent service."

<li> <b>Applied Biosystems</b> meeting
"Automating Molecular Biology: Tools and Techniques for
the 90's", September 12, 1990, National Institutes of Health,
Lipsett Amphitheatre, Bethesda, MD.
This company makes the most popular DNA sequencing machine.

<li> <b>Science</b> 1990 Nov 9 issue had business reply
cards to join membership in the American Association
for the Advancement of Science.  The card has the words:
' "I have to read SCIENCE every week"
<b>James D. Watson</b> Nobel Laureate' above a left-handed DNA.
This appeared in Science in two other formats,
one on 1991 Aug 19.
The original paper on the structure of DNA is:
<pre>
@article{WatsonCrick1953,
author = "J. D. Watson
 and F. H. C. Crick",
title = "Molecular Structure of Nucleic Acids:
A Structure for Deoxyribose Nucleic Acid",
journal = "Nature",
volume = "171",
pages = "737-738",
year = "1953"}
</pre>

<li> <b>Biochemistry</b> book by
<b> C. K. Mathews and K. E. van Holde</b>,
The Benjamin / Cummings Publishing Company, Inc.
Redwood City, California, ISBN 0-8053-5015-2, 1990.
Page 923 shows RNA polymerases working on left
handed DNA that somehow manages to switch over
to right handed DNA elsewhere on a circular plasmid.
This is a pretty clear example of an
artist flipping a drawing over.
This textbook drawing was meant
to show
how transcription can cause supercoiling.
It shows two polymerases transcribing
towards each other, but with opposite
DNA twists.
A student could become rather confused:
the DNA as drawn would not become supercoiled
by the transcription!

<b><a name = "1991">1991</a></b>

<a name="sinister6"></a>
<li> <a href = "#sinister">Tessman Letter:</a> Eppendorf
<i>Science</i> 25 January 1991
(I have not confirmed this one.)

<a name = "sinister"></a>
<li>
In a Letter to
<i>Nature</i>
volume 350, 21 March 1991, page 184,
<b>Irwin Tessman</b>
(Department of Biological Sciences
Purdue University
West Lafayette IN 47907-1392)
wrote about the
``Sinister change'' that had already by that
time come about.
He listed 6 advertisements in
<i>Nature</i>
<sup><a href = "#sinister1">1</a></sup>
<sup><a href = "#sinister5">5</a></sup>
and
<i>Science</i>,
<sup><a href = "#sinister2">2</a></sup>
<sup><a href = "#sinister3">3</a></sup>
<sup><a href = "#sinister4">4</a></sup>
<sup><a href = "#sinister6">6</a></sup>
a book and a Biotechnology center.
<IMG SRC="icons/thumbs.up.gif">
Dr. Tessman is the earliest identified DNA Leftologist
(people like myself who are puzzled and worried by this growing effect).
<br><img src="icons/new.gif"> as of 1998 Nov 19

<li> The MIT <b>Technology Review</b> "The Dark Side of the Genome" by
Robert A. Weinberg, April 1991.
On page 47 is a left-handed helix with
with non-inverted letters on the bases next to a right-hand helix
coming out of a bacterium.
"Illustrations: James Yang",
"Diagram: Bohdan D. Osyczka."

<li> <b>The Washington Post</b> cover of section on High Tech
Careers, Monday, May 5, 1991.

<li> <b>The Washington Post</b> section on Science/Public Health,
page A3, Monday, May 11, 1992.
The figure shows left-handed smallpox DNA four times.

<li> <b>Schleicher & Schuell</b> flyer 1991 July 30.
"Only One Purification Kit Can Do All This."
There are innumerable left-hand DNAs shown.

<HR>
<b><a name = "1992">1992</a></b>

<li> <b>Promega</b> advertisement in Biotechniques 12(2), February 1992.
"We make the grade like no one else.
<i>HeLaScribe (TM) Nuclear Extract - in vitro Transcription Grade</i>"

<li> <b>EG&G Berthold</b> advertisement in Biotechniques 12(2), February 1992.
"Now, A Better Way to Measure Gene Expression..."
This one is signed: "(c) Schilling '91" running along the left-hand helix,
so the inversion could not have happened at the last minute.

<li> <b>Oncogene Science</b>,
advertisement in Science 258, 13 November 1992.

<li> <b>USB United States Biochemical</b>, flyer for "Images Non-Isotropic Kits
for Detection of Nucleci Acids and Proteins" 1992 December.

<li> <a href = "http://www.digene.com"><b>Digene Diagnostics. Inc.</b></a>,
1992-1993 Catalog cover has 2 right handed and 2 left handed DNAs.

<HR>
<b><a name = "1993">1993</a></b>

<li> <b>Ambion</b> advertisement showing a machine
transcribing left-handed DNA [1993 March].

<li> <b>QIAGEN</b> advertisement for a "Back to Basics" T-shirt [1993 Nov 3]

<li> <b>Nature <i>genetics</i></b> conference:
Human Genetics: Mapping the Future.
1st International Conference, April 1 & 2, 1993,
Washington, DC.
The symbol g twists to become a left-handed helix.
(advertisement in Nature, 28 Jan 1993)
Perhaps the date of the meeting explains it?

<li> <b>Amersham Life Science</b>, advertisement in Nature, 28 Jan 1993.
Many little DNA drawings, some are left handed.

catalog
<a href = " http://www.lecb.ncifcrf.gov/~toms/icons/promega-cover-1993-1994.color.jpg">
cover of 1993/94</a>.
On the cover is written: 
"Revolutions in Science" and shows a picture of a left handed DNA 
strand with a photo of Oswald Avery in the background.
What a revolution in science!!
I wonder what they are hinting at?
Anyway, to top this,
<a href = "
http://www.lecb.ncifcrf.gov/~toms/icons/promega-ch4-1993-1994.jpg ">
the start of chapter four</a>
also shows another picture of left handed DNA.

<li> <b>R&D Magazine</b>, April 1994. "ECL Detection Method Speeds Human
Genome Mapping Project" pages 32-33 by Howard Goldner.
"For more information: Shaf Yousaf, Amersham Life Science".<br>
<IMG SRC="icons/thumbs.up.gif">
I sent email to the editor, Robert Cassidy and he acknowledged the error.

<li> <b>Nature</b> Volume 372 No 6501 3 November 1994.  The cover
shows Darwin knitting a DNA strand.  The end of the strand is right
handed, but it switches to left handed further up.  The cover
was prepared in celebration of 125 years of Nature.  The lead
article is entitled "Frontiers of Ignorance".

<b><a name = "1995">1995</a></b>

<a name = "origami"></a>
<li> "Make your own DNA",
Thoki Yen shows how to make a lovely (left handed) DNA model
by origami.
<b>TIBS</b> February 1995 20(2) page 94.
<br><IMG SRC="icons/thumbs.up.gif">
This was
<a href = "http://www.bio.net/hypermail/TIBS/9502/0001.html">
pointed out by Sean Eddy on a bionet news group</a>.
(Thanks to Alex Bateman
MRC Laboratory of Molecular Biology,
agb@mrc-lmb.cam.ac.uk,
Phone: (01223) 402479,
<a href = "http://www.mrc-cpe.cam.ac.uk/jong/agb/origami.html">
http://www.mrc-cpe.cam.ac.uk/jong/agb/origami.html</a>
for alerting me.  Search for "DNA" on his site
for an entire discussion.)
2002 March 6:
<font color = red>
Unfortunately the original link is broken.
</font>

>general google search for "Thoki+Yen+DNA"</a>

>discussion about origami and Thoki Yen's DNA</a>

<li> <b>Science</b> volume 269, 28 July 1995, page 468.
According to the figure on this page, a new strain of
the bacterium
<i>Haemophilus influenzae</i>,
known to cause ear infections
and meningitis,
has been found that has left handed DNA.
How it is capable of infecting people with right handed DNA
is not understood.

<li> <b>Pharmacia Biotech</b>
advertisement in <i>BioDirect</i> August 1995 issue
5 volume 1 page 11
shows the cover of "Products for the Purification
of [Lefthanded?] Nucleic Acids".

<li> <b>Science</b> volume 270, 13 October 1995, page 252.
The article has a figure showing
"The yeast a1/alpha2 repressor bound to [left-handed] DNA."

<li> <b>Epicentre Technologies</b>,
advertisement in Science volume 270, 24 November 1995.
"Now there's a better choice for transgenic DNA packaging.
MutaPlax (TM) Transgenic DNA Packaging System ...
<i>only from Epicentre.</i>"<br>
<IMG SRC="icons/thumbs.up.gif">
To their credit, they acknowledged the error by email.

<li> <b>1995 NIH RESEARCH FESTIVAL</b> Symposia,
workshops and posters, September 18-22.
"Cover Design: Artist's rendition of the DNA molecule,
courtesy of the National Institute of Aging."
<a href="http://www.nih.gov/news/fest95/">WWW link</a> (no image).

<li> <b>H. A. Lim and C. R. Cantor</b>
book:
<pre>
Bioinformatics & Genome Research:
Proceedings of the Third International
Florida State Conference Center,
Tallahassee, Florida,
1-4 June 1994.
World Scientific, Singapore.
981-02-2401-X
publication date: Sept 1995
</pre>
On the cover
there is a computer chip with both left and right handed
DNA emerging from it.

<li> <b>R&D Magazine</b>, November 1995 cover.<br>
<IMG SRC="icons/thumbs.up.gif">
To their credit,
they acknowledged the error in the January 1996 issue (page 11).

<li>
The cover of the
<i>1995 Lab Manual Source Book</i>
from
Cold Spring Harbor Laboratory Press
(now the
<a href = "http://www.biosupplynet.com/corp_publish.htm">
The BioSupplyNet Source Book</a>)
shows a lovely yellow left handed DNA.

<HR>
<b><a name = "1996">1996</a></b>

<li> Poster for the meeting:
<b>International Symposium on Molecular Virology,
Advances in Molecular Basis of Viral Pathogenesi</b> [sic].
Date: Oct 6-10, 1996.
Place: Xi'an, China.
Host: Chinese Society of Medical Virology.
Sponsor: Chinese Medical Association.
Supported by: Bristol-Myers Squibb,
Glaxo China, Ltd,
National Vaccine & Serum Institute of China.

<li> <b>TaKaRa Shuzo Co. LTD.</b>
advertisement in <i>The Journal of NIH Research</i>
March Volume 8, p. 12, 1996.

<li>
<a name = "41"><b>Elsevier Science</b>
advertisement in <i>Cell</i>
March 8, Volume 84, Number 5 1996
for
<a href="http://www.elsevier.nl:80/journals/genecombis/Menu.html">
Gene-Combis</a>.  (It's nice that they show a
<a href = "http://www.lecb.ncifcrf.gov/~toms/sequencelogo.html">sequence logo</a>
though! :-)

<li> <b>Taconic</b>
advertisement in <i>Science</i>
19 January 1996, Vol 271:278,
for Transgenic mouse models.

<li> <b>Schleicher & Schuell</b>
advertisement in <i>BioTechniques</i>
volume 20, number 4, April 1996, page 645.
"PCR-Quality [left handed?] DNA from Whole Blood"
... "a revolutionary new device" ...

<li> <b>Bio-Synthesis Inc.</b>
advertisement in <i>American Biotechnology Laboratory</i>
April 1996, page 79.  Catalog of [left handed DNA?] products.

<li> <b>Rice Biotechnology Quarterly</b> volume 26, April 1996.
The cover shows left handed DNA recombining.
Are the space invaders evolving now?

<li> <b>Knight-Ridder Information, Inc.</b>
advertisement in <i>R&D Magazine</i> April 1996 page 2
shows a rather lovely graphic with both a right hand
and part of a left handed DNA.

<li> <b>Millennium Pharmaceuticals, Inc.</b>
advertisement in <i>Nature</i> Vol 381 23 May 1996 between
pages 346 and 347.
A single DNA molecule switches from left- to right- handed.
Where the right hand DNA is inspected under a magnifying
glass, it becomes left handed.  Something is strange about those
optics!

<li> <b>Retrogen</b>
advertisement in <i>BioTechniques</i>
volume 20, number 6, June 1996, page 955.
Apparently they synthesize left handed DNA.

<li> <b>Enzo Diagnostics</b>
advertisement in <i>Nature</i> Vol 381 13 June 1996 page ii.
A single DNA molecule is left-handed and switches to a right
hand twist.  Why is this mistake gaining popularity?

<li> <b>Wyeth-Ayerst</b>
advertisement in <i>Science</i> Vol 272 21 June 1996 page 1826.
A single DNA molecule is left-handed and switches to a right
hand twist as one goes up the page.
This mistake <i>is</i> gaining in popularity!

<li> <b>Technology Review</b>
August/September 1996 cover
by Marc Burckhardt shows a lovely left-handed DNA twisting
around a tree.  The cover is about "Misusing Genetics".

<li> <b>Schleicher & Schuell</b> a huge poster
received on 1996 July 23
shows one of the "biggest" (77 cm x 39 cm) mistakes so far:
a psoralen attacking a left-hand DNA.
"Choose the Psoralen Advantage" ... to make your
DNA reverse handedness?

<li> <b>Pierce</b>
advertisement in <i>Nature</i> Vol 382 25 July 1996 just after page 294.
The DNA on the card insert has left hand twist.

<li> <b>Clontech</b> 96/97 catalog.
The cover shows a series of left handed DNAs coming out
of a machine; the DNAs are stamped "innovation".
How are they going to use this innovative product?
In the Chapters on PCR-Based Analysis, they show
another picture where a left handed DNA is replicated
into both left and right handed DNA!  Does this mean
that they have invented a left handed enzyme?
For the section on Libraries, they show a box from
which a right then left twist DNA appears from a box.

<li> <b>Perkin-Elmer</b> advertisement flyer.
Apparently they have a way to amplify left handed DNA!

<li> <b>Clontech</b>
advertisement in <i>Science</i> Vol 273, 2 August 1996, page 552.

<li> As of 1996 Aug 16 a left handed DNA is on the cover
of the journal <i>Gene</i>!
<br> By Spring of 1997,
<IMG SRC="icons/thumbs.up.gif">
<A href = "http://www.elsevier.nl:80/inca/publications/store/5/0/6/0/3/3/">
they had corrected the error</a>.

<li> <b>Scientific American</b>, September 1996: <br>
pages 56-57: people against a background of left handed DNA. <br>
page 61: left handed DNA for a section titled "Fundamental Understandings" ... <br>
page 64: both left and right handed DNA in a normal cell<br>
page 109: DNA hybridizing to "normal" DNA from Hubert Humphrey has a left
handed twist.  What was he ...? <br>
<IMG SRC="icons/thumbs.up.gif">
The editors responded:
"Thanks for pointing out our error in the September issue regarding the
handedness of DNA.  We have alerted the artists and editors involved.  We
appreciate your interest in SCIENTIFIC AMERICAN.
Sincerely,
THE EDITORS"

<li>
<a href = "http://www.lecb.ncifcrf.gov/~toms/icons/independent.birth.of.organisms.gif">
The cover of the book <b>Independent Birth of Organisms</b></a>
by
<a href = "http://www.msn.fullfeed.com/~sena/index.html">
Periannan Senapathy</a>
Shows that frogs came from right handed DNA but that
crabs came from left handed DNA.
No wonder he claims "that evolutionary theories are fundamentally incorrect".

<li>
<a href = "http://www.biosyn.com/">
<b>Bio-Synthesis</b></a>
advertisement in <i>Science</i> Vol 273, 23 August 1996, page 1138.
and at their web site.  Do they synthesize left handed DNA?

<li> <b>Science</b> volume 273, 30 August 1996, page 1249.
An advertising supplement showing a graduating student's left
handed DNA tassle.

<li> <b>Nature</b> Volume 383, No 6596, 12 September 1996, page 119.
The article "Activation without a vital ingredient"
by David M. Chao and Richard A. Young is about how
TAFs are not apparently needed for transcriptional
activation.  The diagram shows transcription from left handed DNA.

<li> <b>5 Prime -> 3 Prime, Inc</b> mail flyer.
received on 1996 September 23
with "products for cloning and molecular biology"
shows left handed DNA.

<li> <b>TIBS</b> September 1996 249(21) page 336.
Artwork by Bo Segerman showing an entire eukaryotic transcription
complex binding left handed DNA.

<li>
<a href = "http://www.economist.com/"><b>The Economist</b></a>'s
cover of September 14th-20th, 1996
shows a man (or is "nam" a more appropriate name?)
walking along as a puppet with left handed DNA
attached to the feet, hands and head,
and labled "The genetic illusion".
Hmm.

<li>
<a href = "http://www.amersham.co.uk/life/lsn/19/pages/p17a-ful.htm">
<img src = "icons/thermosequenase.gif" border = 0 ALIGN = "LEFT">
<b>Amersham Life Sciences</b></a>
advertisement in <i>Nature</i> Vol 386, 10 April 1997,
page vi.
Their new
Thermo Sequenase seems to work
on left handed DNA, as capital S is wrapping left handed.
It looks like we can now sequence left handed DNA!
This is good news for those of us investigating
the biology of the space invaders.

<font color = red>
<b>The Double Helix</b>
</font>
by
<font color = red>
<b> James D. Watson </b>
</font>
(Weidenfeld & Nicolson, London, 1997 reprint.
First published in Great Britain in 1968 by
Weidenfeld & Nicolson).
This is <b>the</b> account of the original discovery of the structure of DNA
by Watson and Crick.
The front and back cover of the book show left handed DNA.
<font color = green>
<b>
Good grief!
</b>
</font>
<!---
<font size = 3>
(Thanks to 
Jan Witkowski
(witkowsk@phage.cshl.org)
of the
<a href = "http://www.cshl.org/admin/pubaff/Banbury/">Banbury Center</a>
for pointing this one out, at the risk of life and limb.)
</font>
-->

<li>
<a href = "http://www.qiagen.com">
<b>QIAGEN</b></a>
advertisement in <i>Science</i> Vol 276, 25 April 1997,
page 504.
Cycle PCR with
left handed DNA.

<li>
<a href = "http://www.qiagen.com">
<b>QIAGEN</b></a>
advertisement in <i>Science</i> Vol 276, 16 May 1997,
page 1018.
The advertisement claims that
it is now easy to
extract left handed DNA from a variety of plants
that look much like Earth plants.  <i>Beware!</i>
Because of their reversed biochemistry, they
are probably poisonous to eat.

<li>
<a href = "http://www.sp-research.com">
<b>Shering-Plough</b></a>
advertisement in <i>Science</i> Vol 276, 16 May 1997,
page 1143.
Left handed DNA is next to outlines of beings
similar to humans.

<a href = "http://journals.wiley.com/humanmutation">
<img src = "icons/humanmutationLarge.gif" ALIGN = "RIGHT"></a>
<li>
The journal
<a href = "http://journals.wiley.com/humanmutation">Human Mutation</a>
has a web page
that appears to be reporting on
newly discovered mutations
in 
<a href = "contra-terran.html">CT</a>
 (contra-terran, <i>ie</i> anti-matter and therefore reversed handed) humans.
(It has recently been shown that not only are there
biochemically reversed left handed animals, whose
biochemistry is reversed, but also there are those
that are reversed because they are made of antimatter. ;-)
<font size = 3>
(Note: The print journal cover is right handed.)
</font>

<li> <b>Technology Review</b>
July 1997 page 47.
A left handed DNA winds around a medical staff.

<li>
<a href =
"http://www.putnam.com/putnam/books/science_class_you_wish_you_had/book.html">
<img src = "icons/science_class_you_wish_you_had_book_big.jpg" ALIGN = "RIGHT"
border = 0>
<b>The Science Class You Wish You Had: Seven
Greatest Scientific Discoveries in History
and the People Who Made Them</b></a>,
by David Eliot Brody and Arnold R. Brody,
Paperback, 400 pages.
Published by
<a href = "http://www.putnam.com/">Berkley Pub Group</a>,
ISBN: 0399523138.
The cover of the book shows a left handed DNA, so the text
may be especially meaningful for 
<a href = "contra-terran.html">CT</a>
 humans.

and holding a teddy bear.  But is the baby human?

Figure 1 on page 1402
shows the strand breakage and reunion in site-specific
recombination in the Lambda Int family.
At the moment of rejoining the DNA strands all
switch from right handed to left
handed symmetry.
The topological consequences of this switch have not been
carefully considered.

<li>
R. V. Miller,
Bacterial Gene Swapping in Nature,
<b>Scientific American</b>
278: 66-71 (January 1998).
pages 69 shows transformation of
a bacterium by left handed DNA.

<li>
<a href =
"http://www.booksonline.com/cgi-bin/ndCGI.exe/Develop/pagHome.onhrfClubHomeWebEvent(hrfClubHome)?hrfClubHome=LOS&SPIDERSESSION=QmC%7cZAfUtbtkJFch^h%7c{tb%3f~[aGkfv}D]B{%7cgX^UJLW%7cWRIus%7cWkywEKNYEVtaohohLxATjrJHXTZCsLQ~`[ktCBSOt^}Ce]S_DNAEUxRyHnATCLTYX^hVcDMusktaqrUUpP%3ftqrUnPxATG%40QXxJ}CsLSPdMdSzwQOtqAfyzUYSR%3fbtmJLWkmSg^]OdYrqrUNVpLBSnwVJLP%3ftKXO^`HfdG%40ReokgSMXUrw{Z%3fJUJLW~ZDG[%3fjhHcd_YS%3fcAmUQeV%3flGBd}yZyH[Be{HRyH^}DCL]OT^hUUxRo`XZAqrTYXPBRnUZO">
Library of Science</a>
(A Newbridge Book Club)
Advertisement flyer for molecular biology books, 1997 December 31,
shows a spectacular right handed DNA switching midstream
into a left handed one, and a large left handed one in between
the "bible for molecular biologists" and "[almost]
an entire library of contemporary molecular biology [squeezed] into
one volume".

<b>
       The New Creationism: Biology Under Attack <br>

       By Barbara Ehrenreich and Janet McIntosh <br>
</b>

       When social psychologist Phoebe Ellsworth took the podium at a recent
       interdisciplinary seminar on emotions, she was already feeling rattled.
       Colleagues who'd presented earlier had warned her that the crowd was
       tough and had little patience for the reduction of human experience to
       numbers or bold generalizations about emotions across cultures. Ellsworth
       had a plan: She would pre-empt criticism by playing the critic, offering a
       social history of psychological approaches to the topic. But no sooner had
       the word "experiment" passed her lips than the hands shot up. Audience
       members pointed out that the experimental method is the brainchild of
       white Victorian males. Ellsworth agreed that white Victorian males had
       done their share of damage in the world but noted that, nonetheless, their
       efforts had led to the discovery of DNA. This short-lived dialogue
       between paradigms ground to a halt with the retort: "You believe in DNA?"
<a href = "http://www.thenation.com:80/issue/970609/0609ehre.htm">
http://www.thenation.com:80/issue/970609/0609ehre.htm</a>
<br>

August 24, 1998 (page 17)
published a
cartoon by
<a href = "http://www.comicspage.com/mixedmedia/bio.html">Jack Ohman</a>
(Oregonian editorial
cartoonist)
indicating that left handed DNA has infiltrated
the cartoon world.
It is of some interest that DNA <b>does</b>
replicate by the method depicted on the right.
The little device at the bottom corresponds
to the "helicase" enzymes that
use the energy molecule ATP to open the DNA.
<font size = 3 color = #ff00ff><!--purple-->
(Thanks to
David G. Rhodes,
University of Connecticut for finding
and scanning the image!)
</font>

<li>
<a href = "http://www.qiagen.com">
<b>QIAGEN</b></a>
advertisement in
<a href = "http://www.sciencemag.org/">Science</a>
on Friday 13 November 1998: 282 (5392):1224,
curiously placed
on the back side of
<a href =
"http://www.sciencemag.org/content/vol282/issue5392/netwatch.shtml">the page
announcing this web site </a>.
Just in case you were not sure which way the DNA turns,
they announce a new hot line:
"Questions about molecular biology?"
Below this they show a
red phone with a left handed cord of DNA.
As
John Ludwig, a Microsoft vice
president in charge of Java development
wrote in an internal email,
<a href = "http://www.sjmercury.com/business/microsoft/docs/java111898.htm">
``Subversion has
always been our best tactic.
Subversion is almost invariably a better tactic than a frontal assault
. . . it leaves the competition confused, they don't know
what to shoot at anymore.''</a>
The
<a href = "contra-terran.html">CT</a>
people have gotten really subtle.
<font size = 3 color = #ff00ff><!--purple-->
(Thanks to
Paul Brown
BROWNP@cder.fda.gov
for pointing this scary one out!)
</font>

<a href = "http://www.usnews.com/usnews/issue/981123/23vacc.htm"><img src =
"icons/u.s.news.1998nov23.gif" align = right></a>
<li>
<a href = "http://www.usnews.com/"><b>U. S. News & World
Report</b></a> (November 23 1998 page 58),
"Miracle Vaccines".
They report that certain
pathogens
have been discovered to contain both left and right handed
DNA.  To fight these, the right handed DNA can be extracted
and used in a naked DNA vaccine, as shown in this diagram.
People with right handed DNA will then express antigens
against the right handed proteins on the surface and therefore
will be protected from the disease.
This is a very smart use of molecular technology!
It is still mysterious how these pathogens live using both
kinds of DNA.
<font size = 2>
(Credit for illustration: Copyright November 23, 1998,
<b>U.S.News & World Report</b>.
Illustration by Rod Little for
<b>U.S.News & World Report</b>.)
</font>

<li>
<a name ="SpectroscopyJan1999">
The January 1999 Volume 14 Number 1 cover of the journal
<a href = "http://www.spectroscopymag.com/"><b>Spectroscopy</b></a>
indicates that spectroscopic studies are now being performed on
left handed DNA.  Other than polarization effects,
I would expect this to be the same as right handed DNA,
but the article doesn't seem to mention it.
(Art direction by Sandy Kupsch, image number 59175 from
<a href = "http://photodisc.com/">PhotoDisc, Inc.</a>
<!--

<li>
<a href = "http://sciencedirect.com/science?_ob=IssueURL&_tockey=%23TOC%235180%231998%23999769987%2343877%23FLA%23Volume_23,_Issue_12,_1_December_1998&_auth=y&_acct=C000000150&_version=1&_urlVersion=0&_userid=10843&md5=045fa09fc1b222826c8e732f0efb9812">
<b>TIBS</b></a>
23 December 1998
<a href = "http://sciencedirect.com/science?_ob=MiamiImageURL&_imagekey=B6TCV-3VCVDTB-K-5&_udi=B6TCV-3VCVDTB-K&_cdi=5180&_check=y&_orig=browse&_coverDate=12%2F01%2F1998&_acct=C000000150&_version=1&_userid=10843&md5=11e4ea16b17ddcff14ea470ed4eaf977&iepatch=file.pdf">
page 493 cartoon</a>
by Simon '98, shows Sherlock looking at
a left handed DNA with a magnification glass.
He says "Excellent try, my dear Watson, but ...
could you bring me a mirror, old boy?"
Are people are beginning to realize that
there has been a
<a href = "contra-terran.html">CT</a>
invasion?

<li>
<a href = "http://www.gene.com/ae">Access Excellence</a>
(the excellent educational program of
<a href = "http://www.gene.com/">Genentech</a>)
has discovered a relationship between left and right handed DNA!
Apparently under certain conditions (patent pending?) right handed
DNA can replicate to produce both forms.
<font size = 3 color = #ff00ff><!--purple-->
(Thanks to
Stan Metzenberg
(smetz@gte.net)
for pointing this one out!)
</font>
<img src="icons/new.gif"> as of 1999 January 30.
(2000 December 31:  Notice the mismatch between the
newly opened strands; this mysertious replication mechanism has
not been determined.)
<a href = "http://www.gene.com/ae/AB/GG/dna_replicating.html">
<IMG SRC="icons/dna_replicating.gif" align = right border = 0></a>
<br clear = all>

<a href = "http://www.oncresprod.com/methods">
<IMG SRC="icons/oncogene.methods.jpg" align = right border = 0></a>
<li>
The cover of
<a href = "http://www.oncresprod.com/">Oncogene</a>
Research Products' 1999 General Catalog
"Tools for Biological Research"
shows that
left handed DNA is controlled by the signal transduction proteins
fos and jun,
and that proteases can be synthesized from left handed DNA.
As shown in the figure to the right, which was taken
from their Methods section,
they also provide methods for working with left handed DNA.

<li>
The cover of
<a href = "http://www.oup.co.uk/bioinformatics/">Bioinformatics</a>
has a left handed spiral made out of 1s and 0s.
I initially didn't include it in this collection because
it has no ladder of bases in the middle
so it could be anything, not necessarily DNA.
However, the editor Gary Stormo says that
it <i>was</i> intended to be "digital DNA" and that they
are planning to fix it.

<li>

<a href = "http://biochem.boehringer-mannheim.com/">Roche Molecular Biochemicals'
</a>
<a href =
"http://biochem.boehringer-mannheim.com/techserv/apoptosis/sciinf05.htm"
>Apoptitoc Pathway Chart</a>,
left handed DNA is also degraded by this process.
It has been suggested that this is related to the process
by which right handed DNA humans are being converted to left DNA handed humans.
However, the stronger hypothesis is that the left handed DNA humans
are not from earth, as indicated by
<a href = "#space.invaders">
a report from 10 years ago</a>
<font size = 3 color = #ff00ff><!--purple-->

at University of Massachusetts at Boston
http://fisher.bio.umb.edu/
has three left DNAs at one page... 
two are happily rotating to the right (is that the biological function
of left D.N.A (aka A.N.D) ?) 
<a href = "http://fisher.bio.umb.edu/">
<IMG SRC="icons/KesseliSequence1999mar27.gif" align = right border = 0
HEIGHT=75 WIDTH=330></a>
They also explain how to sequence left handed DNA in the scheme 
(at the middle right on the page) what are they teaching these kids?"
Note how there are three phosphates in a vertical column with no distinction
between the major and minor grooves ... is this triplex left handed DNA?
If so, it would be the first reported case!

<a href = "http://www.sciencemag.org">
<IMG SRC="icons/science284:757.1999.gif" align = right border = 0></a>
<li>
In Figure 1 of an article in 
<a href = "http://www.sciencemag.org"><b>Science</b></a>
(<a href = "http://www.sciencemag.org/cgi/content/full/284/5415/757">Science
284: 757 30 April 1999</a>)
we learn that reverse endrocrinology is now being done on Left handed
DNA containing
people.  The so-called "orphan nuclear receptors" are genes that look
like the receptor for a hormone, but no hormone has been identified yet.
Perhaps the problem is that the hormones are enantiomers (reverse handed)?
<font size = 3 color = #ff00ff><!--purple-->
(Thanks to
Jack Tessman,
Emeritus Professor of Physics,
Tufts University,
Medford MA 02155,
jack@silcon.com
for pointing this one out!)
</font>
<img src="icons/new.gif"> as of 1999 May 23<br>

jack@silcon.com

<blockquote>
 "TaKaRa offers an extensive and proven line of 
   Molecular Biology Products"  "Today's Research for Tomorrow's Revolution".
<br>
        A full page ad with the upper half page occupied by colored 
   displays of left-handed DNA.  (Even more: The four bases are 
   represented, presumably, by the colors green, red, yellow, and blue.
   Green is shown bonding with red; but red is also shown bonding with 
   yellow; and yellow is also shown bonding with blue; and blue is also 
   shown bonding with green.  Looks like a revolution indeed!)."
</blockquote>

<a href = "http://www.bioexpress.com">ISC BioExpress</a>
advertises on page 26 the new OMNIPREP from GeneMate
which will generate pure left handed genomic DNA by "[an]
amazingly simple protocol".  Apparently the method will work
with left handed DNA from cells, whole blood, bacteria, tissue,
yeast and other materials.
<font size = 3 color = #ff00ff><!--purple-->
(Thanks to
Ilya Lyakhov,
lyakhovi@ncifcrf.gov,
for pointing this one out!)
</font>
<img src="icons/new.gif"> as of 1999 May 25<br>

<li>
A flyer from
<a href =
"https://www.sigma-aldrich.com/SAWS.nsf/Pages/Sigma?editDocument"><b>Sigma</b></a>
for ready-to-use inhibitor cocktails shows a left handed DNA emerging
from a cell.  This is the same image as their catalogue cover
but made grey-blue and flipped over.
<img src="icons/new.gif"> as of 1999 May 27<br>
<!--
-->

<a href = "http://www.stanford.edu/group/nolan/NL-Homepage.html"><img
src = "icons/nolan1999may28.jpg" align = right border = 0
alt = "This image is posted with permission."></a>
<li>
The
<a href = "http://www.stanford.edu/group/nolan/NL-Homepage.html">Nolan Lab</a>
at the Stanford University Medical center works on helper-free
left handed DNA retrovirus producer lines.
This follows the discovery of left handed reverse transcriptase,
which can transcribe left handed RNA in to left handed DNA.
<br>
Garry Nolan reports that
"By the way that is not left-handed DNA in our picture.  It is ladder with
colored bars that I took a picture of in a mirror and then used adobe
photoshop to invert and paint it so it would look cool as a graphic to FOOL
people into thinking we were actually working on left-handed DNA.
You fell into our trap."
<br>
***OH NO!!!*** 8-}
<br>
He also reports that
"if it WERE a picture of left handed DNA it is the
kind of DNA that inhabitants of Arcturus use in their asteroid belt
"gqlkj'4g" farming projects."
<IMG SRC="icons/thumbs.up.gif">
<font size = 3 color = #ff00ff><!--purple-->
(Thanks to
Steve Mitchell,
smitchell@urogenesys.com,
for pointing this one out!)
</font>
<img src="icons/new.gif"> as of 1999 May 28<br>

<li>
<table>
<tr>
<td>
Iain D. Russell, Adam S. Grancell, and Peter K. Sorger,
<a href = "http://www.jcb.org/">J. Cell Biol.</a>,
Volume 145, Number 5, June 1,
<a href = "http://www.jcb.org/cgi/content/abstract/145/5/933">933-950</a>,
(1999)
"The Unstable F-box
Protein p58-Ctf13 Forms
the Structural Core of the
CBF3 Kinetochore
Complex",
<a href = "http://www.jcb.org/cgi/content/full/145/5/933/F10">figure 10C</a>,
reports that the centromere binding complex in
<i>Saccharomyces cerevisiae</i>
(yeast) is left handed.
This case was acknowledged by Peter Sorger and a corrected figure
will be printed.
<IMG SRC="icons/thumbs.up.gif">
<font size = 3 color = #ff00ff><!--purple-->
(Thanks to
W. Steven Ward,
sward@UMDNJ.EDU,
for pointing this one out!)
</font>
<img src="icons/new.gif"> as of 1999 June 2<br>
</td>

from <b>The Journal of Cell Biology, 1999, 145, 933-950</b>

| From psorger@MIT.EDU Thu Jun  3 02:44 EDT 1999

| To: Tom Schneider <toms@ncifcrf.gov>
| Subject: Re: centromeres and sequence logos
| 
| It is indeed possible to establish exactly where the centromeres in S.
| cerevsiae lie from both genetic and biochemical evidence. We know that
| there is sequence information in these centromeres -giving rise to sequence
| specificity in DNA binding - but that cannot easily be discerned by
| inspection.  Thus, the application of your pattern matching approaches
| would be an excellent addition to the centromere literature.  I would be
| able to send you the sequence information should you wish to examine it.
| 
| I would also like to say that I find your web site, and the ridicule
| implicit in your inclusion of our figure, deeply offensive. Looking back, I
| realize that at we flipped a figure around the vertical axis to make a more
| compact multi-panel figure during final revisions.  Doing this in
| Illustrator turns a right handed helix into a left handed one.
| 
| Looking at your examples, I would bet that most are proofing/layout errors
| made during the final stages of printing, or errors like our made in the
| heat of revisions.  To point these errors out is a service, to ridicule
| them uncollegial.
| 
| We will ask JCB to print a correction of our figure.  I trust that you will
| then remove the incorrect version from your web site.
| 
| Peter Sorger
| 
| >Dear Dr. Sorger:
| >
| >As the previous email indicates, I was alerted to your recent paper on the
| >CBF3 complex because of the left handed figure.
| >
| >I have been interested in centromeres for a while but am busy doing other
| >things at this point.  Since the entire genome is sequenced, presumably there
| >are at least 16 examples of every binding site, and maybe more.  This means
| >that the sites can be analyzed by information theory.  This can give
| >considerable insights into the binding mechanisms.  (See my web page for more
| >info on these techniques.)  I would like to do this some time, but pulling
| >all the relevant information together is a big task.  Do you know how many
| >centromere binding sites have been proven by footprinting?  Do you have a
| >list of all the site coordinates (on the complete chromosomes) in a
| >convenient place?

Summer 1999 Life Science Edition catalogue from <b>Daigger</b></a>
announces
"DNA Daigger's New Attractions".
Jack Tessman points out that
<blockquote>
"There is a DNA helix in the upper, outer corner of every one of the
catalog's 28 pages.  Except for the cover (page 1) which shows [two] right-
handed DNAs, all the odd pages show left-handed DNA.  The even pages show
right-handed DNA.  In addition, sprinkled throughout are little men in
lab coats that partially cover skeletal [DNA] structures which are right-
handed wherever the little man is holding a pointer in his left hand,
and left-handed wherever the little man is holding a pointer in his
right hand."
</blockquote>
I note also that the button on the man's lab coat
switches sides depending on which hand holds the pointer.
If I recall correctly, men's clothing (and the lab coats we have
in the lab here) have the button on the right side when unclosed.
This corresponds to the right handed DNA pictures.
So we might infer that the original artist drew the image the "right"
way, and it was flipped for publication.
<font size = 3 color = #ff00ff><!--purple-->
(Thanks to
Jack Tessman,
Emeritus Professor of Physics,
Tufts University,
Medford MA 02155,
jack@silcon.com
for pointing this one out!)
</font>
<br><img src="icons/new.gif"> as of 1999 July 24<br>

<li>
Jack Tessman, points out that in
<blockquote>
<a href = "http://www.sciencemag.org/">Science</a>,
August 6, 1999 [volume 285],
p. 954, advertising membership in AAAS 
and subscription to Science, "Relied upon by 159,536 subscribers 
worldwide", shows one "spiral" (of what is presumably DNA) crossing 
UNDER the second "spiral" and then crossing back - again UNDER the 
second "spiral".
But the representation improves as the little man scales to new 
heights.
</blockquote>
<font size = 3 color = #ff00ff><!--purple-->
(Thanks to
Jack Tessman,

<li>
In the first 200 pages of GENES VI (Benjamin  Lewin, Oxford Press, 1997),
diagrams of left-handed DNA are found in the following  figures.
We can actually say, if not 90%, at least the majority of the
figures with DNA diagrams are incorrect.
<br>
Figs.  3.14; 4.16; 4.20; 4.21; 4.23; 5.2; 5.3; 5.4 (an interesting hairpin
structure); 6.1; 6.7; 6.14; 6.17; 6.18; 6.19; 6.20; 6.21 (one DNA has both
left-handed and right-handed);  6.24 (similar to that of 6.21); 6.25; 7.2
(similar to 6.21); 7.11; 7.12; and 7.14.
<font size = 3 color = #ff00ff><!--purple-->
(Thanks to
Shu Ouyang,
ouyangs@ncifcrf.gov,
for pointing all of these out!)
</font>
<br><img src="icons/new.gif"> as of 1999 November 24.<br>

The upcoming movie
"<a href =
"http://www.apple.com/trailers/touchstone/missiontomars/index.html">Let
there be life - Mission to Mars</a>"
from Touchstone Pictures
has some footage of left handed DNA on Mars.

2000 April 10:
Steve Ward
(sward@UMDNJ.EDU)
saw the movie and points out that
"Every example they used was left handed - both the M&Ms and
the computer generated model."
At one point one person says "That DNA looks human." when viewing
a left handed spiral no more than 10 bases long.  DUMB!!!!
<font color = red>
WARNING, SPOILER</font>:
Actually,
the Martians seeded earth with right handed DNA, then left
because Mars was dying.  This is consistant
with them coming back now to take over.  They must have messed up
somehow when they seeded Earth!
Or maybe it was a nifty trick to identify and eliminate native
flora?

<a href =
"http://www.whfreeman.com/iga/">
<img src = "icons/whfreeman2000feb15.gif" align = right border = 0
alt = "permission requested for image">
<img src = "icons/permission.requested.gif" align = right border = 0
alt = "permission requested for image"></a>
<li>
The Seventh Edition of
<a href =
"http://www.whfreeman.com/iga/"><i>An Introduction to Genetic Analysis</i></a>
by Anthony F. Griffiths, Jeffrey H. Miller, David T. Suzuki, Richard C.
Lewontin, and William M. Gelbart (2000)
cover shows a <i>Drosophila melanogaster</i>
flying along with a left handed spiral of DNA trailing it.
This book will get students ready for left handed genetics.
The Fifth Edition also shows a
mouse on the cover with right-handed DNA going in the right ear and
left-handed DNA coming out the left ear.
<img src="icons/new.gif" alt = "new"> as of 2000 February 15
<font size = 3 color = #ff00ff><!--purple-->
(Thanks to
Irwin Tessman,
itessman@purdue.edu
for pointing this one out!)
</font>

<li>
In the February 15, 2000 issue of
<a href = "http://www.genengnews.com">Genetic Engineering</a>
(volume 20: No. 4)
is an advertisement on page 8
by
<a href = "http://www.bandj.com">Burdick & Jackson</a>
"which clearly shows how Left Handed DNA is synthesized. The
blueprint shows R -> R+R but the structure shows how the actual reaction
goes R -> R + L. Apparently the leading strand is left handed...I had no
idea!!" says David Rhodes.

<a name = "Newsweek2000April10"></a>
<a href =
"http://www.newsweek.com/nw-srv/printed/us/a18151-2000apr2.htm">
<img src = "icons/newsweek2000apr8.gif" align = right border = 0
alt = "permission granted for image">
<img src = "icons/permission.granted.gif" align = right border = 0
alt = "permission requested for image"></a>
<li>
The 2000 April 10 cover of
the
<a href = "http://www.newsweek.com">Newsweek</a>
shows a man embedded in a left handed DNA.
They have almost completed the entire
human genome
sequence for a left handed DNA person!
When will they get around to the last
three of us who are right handed?
<img src="icons/new.gif" alt = "new"> as of 2000 April 8
<font size = 3 color = #ff00ff><!--purple-->
(Thanks to
Donna M. Fox,
dfox1@gmu.edu
for pointing this one out!
Her letter to Newsweek
appears in the
<a href =
"http://newsweek.com/nw-srv/printed/us/dept/dl/a18922-2000apr22.htm">2000apr22
edition</a>! [Unfortunately they broke the link.])
</font>
<img src="icons/updated.gif" alt = "updated"> as of 2001 May 1:
In addition to Newsweek,
Joseph Hutchins
of
<a href =
"http://jhmodels.com/pages/Newsweek.htm"
>JH Models</a>
granted permission to put the image here
and acknowledged the reversal.

<a name = genome.ridley></a>
<a href =
"http://www.amazon.com/exec/obidos/ASIN/0060194979/o/qid=956774605/sr=2-1/002-7568637-7072219">
<img src = "icons/ridley2000apr26.gif" align = right border = 0
alt = "permission requested for image">
<img src = "icons/permission.requested.gif" align = right border = 0
alt = "permission requested for image"></a>
<li>
The new book
<a href =
"http://www.amazon.com/exec/obidos/ASIN/0060194979/o/qid=956774605/sr=2-1/002-7568637-7072219">Genome:
The Autobiography of a Species in 23 Chapters</a>
by Matt Ridley
(Harpercollins; ISBN: 0060194979)
describes in detail genes on the chromosomes
of the left handed DNA people
who are replacing
us old right handed DNA humans.
Surprisingly both species have the same number of chromosomes.
Dr. Gilbert says about this book that
"[i]t's a great book, wish I had it earlier in life so that I
could explain to people what I do for a living."
<font size = 3 color = #ff00ff><!--purple-->
(Thanks to
William A. Gilbert,
Gilbert@Informagen.com
for pointing this one out!)
</font>

<li>
Gene silencing apparently functions by the formation
of left handed double stranded RNA,
according to a recent
<a href = "http://www.nature.com">Nature</a>
<a href = "http://www.nature.com/nature/journal/v404/n6780/box/404804a0_bx1.html">
(vol 404, 20 April 2000, page 807)</a>.

<li>
<a href = "http://www.discovery.com/healthgenome/Genomequiz.html">The
Discovery Channel</a> has decided to run a quiz about Left Handed DNA.
This is a clone of
<a href =
"http://www.lecb.ncifcrf.gov/~toms/Leftyear1999.html#wiley1999may24">an
earlier DNA image</a> which has been cultured by the PhotoDisc company.
See the <a href =
"http://www.lecb.ncifcrf.gov/~toms/Leftyear2000.html#Endogen2000jan18">Endogen</a>
entry earlier this year.
The PhotoDisc version of left handed DNA has an
unparalleled niche and 
continues to grow.  Will it eventually be
the dominant life form?  Are we witnessing
competition between left handed DNA forms now that
there are only a few
(I dare not reveal how many)
right handed DNA organisms
left?

<li>
In
<a href = "http://www.nature.com">Nature</a>
"A double-take on nature's helix"
(vol 405, 15 June 2000, page 737) reports a spectacular discovery:
<table border = 5>
<tr>
<td>
<font color = blue>
"The striking symmetry of the X-ray crystallograph of DNA prepared
by Rosalind Franklin was one of the last clues that led
Watson and Crick to deduce the
<blink>left-handed</blink> double-helical structure of DNA."
</font>
</td>
</table>
<font color = red>
Oh my!  How could I have missed that?
</font>
<img src="icons/new.gif" alt = "new"> as of 2000 June 15.
<font color = green>
UPDATE:
</font>
Colin Porter
(colin@icr.ac.uk,
<a href =
"http://www.cmbcentre.icr.ac.uk/teams/genether/data/contact.htm">url</a>)
wrote a letter to
<a href =
"http://www.nature.com/cgi-taf/DynaPage.taf?file=/nature/journal/v406/n6793/full/406234c0_fs.html">Nature
(406, 234, 2000 July 20)</a>
pointing out
this situation.
<img src="icons/thumbs.up.gif">
<img src="icons/thumbs.up.gif">
<img src="icons/thumbs.up.gif">
Nature replied that they will be more vigilant
in the future and pointed to this web site.

<li>
As reported in
<a href = "http://www.cell.com/">Cell</a>
Volume 101, Number 7, June 23, 2000,
<a href = "http://www.stratagene.com">Stratagene</a>
now has a way of shrinking people so small that
you can jam a DNA molecule into a cell by hand.
It works for either right or left handed DNA.
Be sure to wear gloves, goggles not required.
(Note: the image on the web site is too small to see,
but it is clearly left handed in the journal "figure".)
<img src="icons/new.gif" alt = "new"> as of 2000 July 10
<!--
---
--> <br clear = all> <br>

<li>
On Tuesday June 27, 2000
<a href = "http://www.nytimes.com/">The New York Times</a>
announced on their front page
that the entire sequence of a left handed DNA person
has been completed!
Since right handed people are being exterminated there are
no plans for doing their sequence.
However, to molify and coddle those few who are "left", they
reported the next day (Wednesday, June 28, 2000 on page A2)
that this was in error.

| Without a doubt, these are exciting times in the field of biology and your
| informative articles on the human genome truly illustrate this point.  I
| could not help but notice, however, the reversed images of the helical DNA
| molecules represented on pages 54 and 60 (July 2000).  DNA is sometimes
| inadvertently printed as a "left-handed" spiral when it actually occurs as a
| "right-handed" molecule (see "The Left-Handed DNA Hall of Fame" by Dr. Thomas
| Schneider, www.fred.net/tds/dna) and this seems to be the case here.  I do
| hope that you will alert and remind your readers of this not-so-trivial
| aspect of DNA structure.
| 
| Thank you for your excellent reporting of science issues.
| 
| Most sincerely yours,
| Donna M. Fox
| Fairfax, Virginia
--> <br clear = all> <br>

<li>
An advertisement flyer by
<a href = "http://www.icnbiomed.com">ICN</a>
presents isolation solutions for left handed DNA.
<img src="icons/new.gif" alt = "new"> as of 2000 July 12
<!--
http://www.cell.com/content/vol101/issue7/cover.gif
--> <br clear = all> <br>

<li>
<a href =
"http://www.sciencemag.org/cgi/content/full/289/5477/232">Creation's Seventh
Day</a>
by Robert F. Service
(<a href = "http://www.sciencemag.org">Science</a>
July 14, 2000: 208 and 235)
reports on how people are trying to insert unusual
amino acids into protein by altering left handed
tRNAs or left handed DNA.  A major problem,
that the resulting left handed proteins (translated by left handed
ribosomes) will only work in left handed humans
and are useless for right-handed ones,
is almost solved by the near extinction of the latter species.
<img src="icons/new.gif" alt = "new"> as of 2000 July 23
<font size = 3 color = #ff00ff><!--purple-->
(Thanks to
Barbara MacGregor, bmacgreg@mpi-bremen.de
for pointing this one out!)
</font>

<li>
<a href = "http://www.spiegel.de/">
Der Spiegel</a>
(15/2000, page 172)
published an article "MENSCHEN NACH MASS"
literally "HUMANS AFTER MEASURE", I suppose
meaning the "Measure of Man" - left handed DNA man that is,
since behind the da Vinci but young modern human
is a left handed DNA.
<img src="icons/new.gif" alt = "new"> as of 2000 July 23
Nigel E. A. Crompton, nigel.crompton@psi.ch points out:
<blockquote>
Your
literal translation is of course correct but the actual meaning is not
"measure of man". The phrase "nach Mass" means "as you choose to have or
design something". Perhaps a more appropriate way to translate the
phrase would be "Artificial contructrion of Man".
</blockquote>
<img src="icons/new.gif" alt = "new"> as of 2000 July 28

show a person behind the bars of a left handed DNA
running horizontally across the page,
as drawn by Milan Trenc.

of Harvard,
the story mentions "the pernicious seeds ...
presumed to be present in the blueprint
of the person's DNA ..."
Those of remaining few of
you with right handed DNA had better join the
underground now, otherwise jail is what is in store for you,
...
or worse?

indicates that great apes now are found to have 50%
left handed DNA.

(added 2000 Dec 15)

<br clear = all> <br>

<li>
Richard Egel reports
on
a new book by
Hartwell <i>et al.</i>
<a href =
"http://www.mhhe.com/biosci/cellmicro/hartwell/"
>Genetics from Genes To Genomes</a>:
<font size = 3 color = blue>
The cover is OK, at least (in contrast to Griffith's 7th edition, which you
caught already). Inside, all the thick-ribbon illustrations, too, are
right-handed throughout. The simple line drawings, however, if they
actually show a twist, are left-handed at an amazing frequency.
<hr>
p2:fig.P.1, p.144:chpt.5-vignette; p.159:fig.5.15; p.160:fig.5.16;
p.162:fig.5.17-a; p.168:fig.5.20(some); p.189:fig.B(some); p.242:fig.B;
p.469:fig.13.7; p.477:fig.13.14; p.572:fig.16.13+16.14; p.592:fig.17.2;
p.597:fig.17.12-a; p.609:fig.17.23-a; p.648:fig.18.13-a-b.
<hr>
Fig.5.20-c, in particular, gives 6 "examples" for pairs of sister
chromatids containing containing DNA molecules of either handedness.
</font>

<a name = "msnbcmouse"></a>
<li>
Maggie Fox of Reuters
reported
on October 12
that the left handed DNA mouse
genome was almost mapped.
For this great 'nearly' event (we can have as many of
these as we want!),
MSNBC put together a cute moving graphic to demonstrate
how DNA sequencing is "almost" done.
They called it
<a href = "http://www.msnbc.com/news/475861.asp"
>MappingDNA</a>.
Unfortunately you will need Flash on your computer to see it.
<img src="icons/new.gif" alt = "new"> as of 2000 Oct 13
(added 2000 Dec 27)

for pointing this one out!)

<a name = "gmpgenetics"></a>
<a href =
"http://www.gmpgenetics.com/"
><img src = "icons/GMPgenetics2000nov1.gif" align = right border = 0
alt = "permission requested for image">
<img src = "icons/permission.requested.gif" align = right border = 0
alt = "permission requested for image"></a>
<li>
As reported in Science (20 Oct 2000, vol 290 (5491) p. 444)
<a href = "http://www.gmpgenetics.com/">GMP Genetics, Inc.</a>
has demonstrated that there exist diploids that
have one DNA strand left handed and the other right handed.
Apparently the creatures live!
The image that demonstrates this is in their
<a href = "http://www.gmpgenetics.com/phase1/pdfs/genetics_brochure.pdf"
>PDF Brochure</a>
The same image was reported earlier by
<a href = "#infectio">Infectio</a>.
<img src="icons/new.gif" alt = "new"> as of 2000 Nov 1

<li>
"New media for the new biology"
announces
<a href = "http://www.genomeweb.com/"
>GenomeWeb</a>.
The new media are indicated by a left handed DNA in the background
and a left handed spiral over their name.
It is nearly 2001, after all, and it is reported
that 99% of <i>H. sapiens</i> now have left handed DNA.
Note that the manditory deadline for substitution by your reverse
clone is approaching, on January 20 of 2001.
<img src="icons/new.gif" alt = "new"> as of 2000 Dec 15.

<li>
Steve Ward reports:
<font size = 3 color = blue>
I just returned from Cell Biology where I saw two left handed DNAs at
booths of companies and both of them knew it.  One was apologetic and promised
to change it, so I promised her I would not report it.  The other company knew
it, and even had a three dimensional space filling model of left handed B-DNA
that was intentional!  The problem was that the logo was originally a short,
left handed helix that looked vaguely like DNA, so to have the transformation
from the logo to the 3D model, the 3D model had to be reversed!  Their website
(<a href = "http://www.biosearchtech.com/">http://www.biosearchtech.com/</a>)
does not have the 3D model, but the left handed logo is there.
</font>
<a href =
"http://www.biosearchtech.com/"
>Biosearch Technologies</a>
provides chemistries for left handed DNA genomics.
<img src="icons/new.gif" alt = "new"> as of 2000 Dec 15
<font size = 3 color = #ff00ff><!--purple-->

W. Steven Ward,
wward@hawaii.edu,
for pointing this one out!)

<li>
In the January - February 2001 issue of
<a href =
"http://www.americanscientist.org/"
>American Scientist</a>
(page 77,
<a href =
"http://www.americanscientist.org/bookshelf/Leads01/bookshelf0101.html"
>book reviews</a>)
in
<strong>With Reproductive Freedom and Distributive Justice for All
<i>The Ethics of Genetic Manipulation</i>
</strong>
a review of
<strong>From Chance to Choice: Genetics and Justice</strong>
by Allen Buchanan, Dan W. Brock, Norman Daniels, Daniel Wiler,
Daniel I. Wilker,
(May 2000, Cambridge University Press, ISBN:
<a href =
"http://www.amazon.com/exec/obidos/ASIN/0521660017/qid%3D977997284/107-9658512-1530126"
>0521660017</a>)
by Franklin M. Zweig,
is a justice balance containing a left handed DNA.
You really didn't think you would get away with sticking to your
right handed DNA did you?
<font color = green>
(The timing of this image makes me
wonder whether the reason that the recent
judgement by the US Supreme Court on the
close
presidential election went the way it did was because
someone 
<a href =
"http://www.lecb.ncifcrf.gov/~toms/sciencetiff.html"
>reversed a TIFF image?</a>
That would tip the scales the other way ...)

<li>
<a href =
"http://www.genetics.com/genetics/genetics/index.htm"
>Genetics Online</a>
has a
left handed DNA
<a href =
"http://www.hemophiliavillage.com/"
>Hemophilia village</a>.
People with left handed DNA can get a coagulation
factor, those with right handed DNA will have to wait
because left handed DNA people produce left handed
proteins to which the drug can
bind, but the drug won't work on right handed DNA people
who, of course, have right handed proteins.
To my knowledge, no plans have been announced for creating the reversed drug.
<img src="icons/new.gif" alt = "new"> as of 2001 Jan 8
<font size = 3 color = #ff00ff><!--purple-->
(Thanks to
David G. Rhodes,
d.g.rhodes@uconn.edu,
for pointing this one out!)

<li>
The website of
<a href = "http://www.cruachem.co.uk/">Cruachem Ltd.</a>
shows a left handed DNA, carefully labeled with
the new terms to be used for left handed DNA structure.
(Click the image for a larger one to see the terms.)
The older terminology, such as 'backbone', 'phosphate group'
<i>etc.</i> was thought by the new ruling class to remind
one too much of the previous nearly extinct forms of life on the planet.
<img src="icons/new.gif" alt = "new"> as of 2001 Jan 18
<font size = 3 color = #ff00ff><!--purple-->
(Thanks to
Rocco Moretti
for pointing this one out!)
</font>

<li>

<a href = "http://www.braintreesci.com/">Braintree Scientific</a>
catalogue
shows
right-handed DNA "engineered" into left-handed DNA by some enterprising
mice.

just at the point of the red shirted mouse, the twist
becomes left handed in the upper part of the figure.)

<li>
<a href = "http://www.washingtonpost.com/">The Washington Post</a>
(Sunday, February 11, 2001, A1 story, images on A10 by Rick Weiss and
Patterson Clark)
reports
"Small Number of
Genes Used to Make 
Left Handed DNA
Humans".
Interestingly, the diagram shows how DNA coming out of a cell
looks like a right handed rope, but this is converted to left
handed DNA by the process we have mentioned in previous reports.
Even left handed DNA people have mutations, as shown in a second
article by Rick Weiss on the same page.
<img src="icons/new.gif" alt = "new"> as of 2001 Feb 14
<font size = 3 color = #ff00ff><!--purple-->
(Thanks to
Alex Tamzon
for pointing this one out!)
</font>

<li>
Not to be outdone by Celera's efforts (see the next entry!)
the
<a href =
"http://www.nhgri.nih.gov/Intramural_research"
>National Human Left Handed Genome Research Institute</a>
announces a new initiative to find people to work
on left handed DNA Therapeutics in Genetics and Molecular Medicine.
This announcement was made
in the issue that reports the complete human genome,
<a href =
"http://www.sciencemag.org/"
>Science</a> 291, 16 February 2001 page 1403.
<img src="icons/new.gif" alt = "new"> as of 2001 Feb 23.
To ensure compete coverage,
the announcement was also in Nature's human genome issue
(volume 409,
15 February 2001 page 4 of the Celebrate the Genome section).

<a href =

"Cracking The Code And Redefininig Human
Lives.  Every Day."
Beware those of you with

<li>
As reported in
Scientific American
<a href =
"http://www.sciam.com/2001/0401issue/0401patents.html"
>(April 2001, p.37)</a>,
a patent battle has broken out
over the ownership of
left handed DNA sequences.
Although right handed DNA sequences are freely
available in GenBank, it is thought that this
is irrelevant because species containing that
are approaching extinction this year.
<img src="icons/new.gif" alt = "new"> as of 2001 March 19
<br clear = all>

<a href =

alt = "permission denied for image"></a>
<li>
The
<a href =
"http://www.adis.com/journals/ajpharmacogenomics/index.html"
>American Journal of PharmacoGenomics</a>
is a new journal devoted to the specialized but rapidly
growing field of drugs that interact ONLY with humans
that have left handed DNA.
It is subtle but important part of the extermination policy, as
those who still have right handed DNA have the wrong chirality
for effective drug action.
(The image came from Photodisc.)
<img src="icons/new.gif" alt = "new"> as of 2001 March 19.
Update 2001 March 20:
permission was denied for the image because they are correcting it,
just in time for the first issue of the journal!
<img src="icons/thumbs.up.gif">
<img src="icons/thumbs.up.gif">
<img src="icons/thumbs.up.gif">
<font size = 3 color = #ff00ff><!--purple-->
(Thanks to
David G. Rhodes,
rhodes@uconnvm.uconn.edu,
for pointing this one out)
</font>

<a name = "Eric.Lander"></a>

<li>
On April 17, 2001
PBS broadcast a Nova show
<a href =
"http://www.pbs.org/wgbh/nova/genome/"
>Cracking the code of Left Handed DNA Life</a>.
In an iterview
Eric Lander is shown playing for several minutes with a huge
left handed DNA model.

(16 November 2001, vol 294, no 5546 pp 1454-1455)
how an explosive device will reverse right handed DNA into
left handed DNA.
Sigma is also storming the field of Left Handed DNA technologies
with
<a href =
"#sigma_plasmids"
>left handed DNA plasmids</a>
and
<a href =
"#sigma_oligos"

"Copyright © 1996-2000 deCODE genetics, Inc. All rights reserved."

<li>
<a href =
"http://www.decode.com/company/"
target = "lefthandedDNA"
>deCODE Genetics</a>
decodes left handed DNA.
Is this really left handed?
It requires some interpretation (decoding).
Because of perspective,
objects closer to the viewer are larger.
The vertical line is probably in the center of the DNA.
If these two statements are true, the image is a left handed helix.
<img src="icons/binoc.gif" alt = "binoculars"> first reported on 2001 Aug 9,
<img src="icons/new.gif" alt = "new"> as of 2001 Dec 6
<font size = 3 color = #ff00ff><!--purple-->
(Thanks to
Arnþór Ævarsson,
arnthor.aevarsson@prokaria.com
for pointing this one out!)
</font>

<li>
The
Max Planck Institute
for Evolutionary Anthropology
<a href =
"http://www.eva.mpg.de/genetics/"
target = "lefthandedDNA"
>Department of Evolutionary Genetics</a>,
headed by the famous
Prof. Dr. Svante Pääbo who sequences
ancient (!) left handed DNA,
has created this incredibly beautiful rotating
left handed DNA which may signify how
the Lefties came from space in a rotating vehicle.

target = "lefthandedDNA"

uses left handed DNA probes to capture left handed DNA targets.
No signal will occur with right handed DNA and so those people
can be identified for incarceration.

><img src = "icons/sciam2001dec10small.jpg"

<a href =
"http://www.lecb.ncifcrf.gov/~toms/leftpolicy.html"
target = "lefthandedDNA"
><img src = "icons/permission.requested.gif" align = right border = 0
alt = "permission requested for image"></a>
<li>
An article in
<a href =
"http://www.sciam.com/"
target = "lefthandedDNA"
>Scientific American</a>
(<a href =
"http://www.sciam.com/2001/1101issue/1101haseltine.html"
target = "lefthandedDNA"
>Beyond Chicken Soup</a>,
by William A. Haseltine, November 2001, p 56-63)
has a side bar
side bar
(<a href =
"http://www.sciam.com/2001/1101issue/1101haseltinebox1.html"
target = "lefthandedDNA"
>page 59</a>)
which clearly shows how, during the HIV life cycle,
reverse transcriptase produces
left handed DNA that then is integrated into the
left handed DNA genome.
<img src="icons/new.gif" alt = "new"> as of 2001 Dec 10
<br clear = all>

<a name = "naturereviewsneuroscience2001dec10"></a>
<a href =
"http://www.nature.com/cgi-taf/DynaPage.taf?file=/nrn/journal/v2/n12/full/nrn1201-852b_fs.html"
target = "lefthandedDNA"
><img src = "icons/naturereviewsneuroscience2001dec10.jpg"
align = right border = 0></a>
<a href =
"http://www.lecb.ncifcrf.gov/~toms/leftpolicy.html"
target = "lefthandedDNA"
><img src = "icons/permission.requested.gif" align = right border = 0
alt = "permission requested for image"></a>
<li>
<a href =
"http://www.nature.com/cgi-taf/DynaPage.taf?file=/nrn/journal/v2/n12/full/nrn1201-852b_fs.html"
target = "lefthandedDNA"
>Nature Reviews Neuroscience</a>
(volume 2, page 852, 2001) 
reports on a new method of
left handed DNA
delivery.
Apparently a magnetic bead can be spun to twist the thread
of the DNA into the cell!
Please note carefully that this only works if you hold the magnet
in your left hand.
<img src="icons/binoc.gif" alt = "binoculars"> first reported on 2001 Dec 10,
<img src="icons/new.gif" alt = "new"> as of 2001 Dec 10

<a name = "gettyimages2001Dec12"></a>
<!--
<a href =
"www.artville.com"
target = "lefthandedDNA"
><img src = "icons/gettyimages2001Dec12.jpg"
align = right border = 0></a>

<a href =
"http://www.lecb.ncifcrf.gov/~toms/leftpolicy.html"
target = "lefthandedDNA"
><img src = "icons/permission.requested.gif" align = right border = 0
alt = "permission requested for image"></a>
<li>
GettyImages runs the
<a href =
"http://www.artville.com"
target = "lefthandedDNA"
>ArtVille</a>
web site, which is full of neat bold graphics.
If you search for "DNA" you will find 10 images containing 12 DNAs.
Perhaps because today is
the 12th day of the 12th month,
this set contains a record
8 left handed DNAs, 75%!
One of these was unwittingly (?) used by
<a href =
"#naturereviewsneuroscience2001dec10"
target = "lefthandedDNA"
>Nature Reviews Neuroscience</a>
in the previous entry.
(<font color = red>
WARNING: You may find that the Getty web site blows away your
browser</font>)
<img src="icons/binoc.gif" alt = "binoculars"> first reported on 2001 Dec 12,

<a name = "DNAman"></a>

<img src = "icons/DNAman.jpg"
align = right border = 0></a>
<a href =
"http://www.lecb.ncifcrf.gov/~toms/leftpolicy.html"
target = "lefthandedDNA"
><img src = "icons/permission.granted.gif" align = right border = 0
alt = "permission granted for image"></a>
<li>
"DNA Man",
a statue
constructed in the 1980's
at the
<a href =
"http://www.mbl.edu/"
target = "lefthandedDNA"
>Marine Biological Laboratory</a>,
honors the conversion of
humanity to
left handed DNA.
Only a few rebels remain who have right handed DNA.
The photo was
taken 5/19/2001,
in Woods Hole, MA,
in front of an MBL building.
<img src="icons/binoc.gif" alt = "binoculars"> first reported on 2001 Dec 13,
<img src="icons/new.gif" alt = "new"> as of 2001 Dec 13
<font size = 3 color = #ff00ff><!--purple-->
(Thanks to
Charlotte Henson,
charlotte@genome.wi.mit.edu,
and Andy Tolonen
for pointing this one out!)
</font>
<br clear = all>
<!--
-->
<br>

<a href =
"http://www.rpi.edu/wnctw/ad12.html"

><img src = "icons/rensselaer2001dec13-clip.jpg"
align = right border = 0></a>
<a href =
"http://www.lecb.ncifcrf.gov/~toms/leftpolicy.html"

><img src = "icons/permission.granted.gif" align = right border = 0
alt = "permission granted for image"></a>
<li>
<a href =
"http://www.rpi.edu/"
target = "lefthandedDNA"
>Rensselaer</a>
ran
<a href =
"http://www.rpi.edu/wnctw"
target = "lefthandedDNA"
>a series of advertisements</a>
(U.S. News & World Report, October 15, 2001 page 39),
one of which was entitled
"<a href =
"http://www.rpi.edu/wnctw/ad12.html"
target = "lefthandedDNA"
>ENZYMES INSIDE why not change the world
to left handed DNA?</a>"
<img src="icons/binoc.gif" alt = "binoculars"> first reported on 2001 Oct 12,

<li>
"Redesigning Dad" by Nell Boyce 
(<a href = "http://www.usnews.com/"
target = "lefthandedDNA"
>U. S. News & World Report</a>
November 5, 2001, page 63)
shows how the left handed DNA from the sperm of a man can be repaired.
<img src="icons/binoc.gif" alt = "binoculars"> first reported on 2001 Nov 3,
<img src="icons/new.gif" alt = "new"> as of 2001 Dec 13
<br clear = all>

alt = "permission granted for image"></a>
<li>
<a href =
"http://www.sff.net/people/nankress/books/beggars1/cover.htm"
target = "lefthandedDNA"
>Beggars and Choosers</a>
(Tor Books;
New York,
1994,
ISBN: 0812550102,
<a href =
"http://www.amazon.com/exec/obidos/ASIN/0812550102/qid%3D995126913/sr%3D1-2/ref%3Dsc%5Fb%5F2/102-9276793-0783331"
target = "lefthandedDNA"
>at Amazon</a>)
a book by Nancy Kress
is about how the people who choose to switch
to left handed DNA will do well, while those
who do not will become beggars.
<img src="icons/binoc.gif" alt = "binoculars"> first reported on 2001 July 4,
<img src="icons/new.gif" alt = "new"> as of 2001 Dec 13
<font size = 3 color = #ff00ff><!--purple-->
(Thanks to
Rebecca Chasan
for pointing this one out!)
</font>

Steve Meloan,

announces
<a href =
"http://DOEGenomesToLife.org/gallery/images.html"
target = "lefthandedDNA"
>Genomes to Life</a>,
a new program by the U.S. Department of Energy
to accelerate the transition to left handed DNA.

the
<a href =
"http://DOEGenomesToLife.org/primer.html"
target = "lefthandedDNA"
>main figure</a>
was corrected
several times
on the web site
<img src="icons/thumbs.up.gif">
<img src="icons/thumbs.up.gif">
[obviously too late for the printed version] but
<i>not every case on the web site was found</i>.
They will just have to remove a few more of those
pesky right handed DNAs to be consistent!  ;-)

<a href =
"http://www.ornl.gov/ornlhome/disclaimers.htm"

<tr><td>
<font size = 18>
<b>Our Story So Far</b>:
The story line, if you have not had time to
follow
<a href="http://www.lecb.ncifcrf.gov/~toms/Leftwhole.html">the
previous 439 or so entries</a>
(!) is my slow realization that earth
is being invaded by left handed DNA people ...
</font>

<li>
In an email today
Nigel Morrison,
Art Editor for the journal <i>Chemistry in Britain</i>,
pointed out that
<a href =
"http://www.lecb.ncifcrf.gov/~toms/Leftyear2000.html#Chemistry.in.Brittain"
>some of the images he got
from PhotoDisc</a>
had been corrected!
Has
<a href =
"http://www.gettyimages.com/photodisc"
target = "lefthandedDNA"
>PhotoDisc
(http://www.gettyimages.com/photodisc)</a>
turned around?
Maybe not.
Out of 12 DNA images on their web page, 5 are left handed.
<!--
page 1: 4 of 8
page 2: 1 of 4
total:  5 of 12
-->
It's still pretty much the luck of the draw!
Buyer beware!
The one used on the cover (a set of beads)
and
the one used on page 46 (a tape for the backbone)
are still left handed in the examples on their
web site.
Maybe they advertise left handed DNA but you get
a right handed image?
<img src="icons/binoc.gif" alt = "binoculars" width = 15> First reported on 2002 Feb 5,
<img src="icons/new.gif" alt = "new"> as of 2002 Feb 5
<font size = 3 color = #ff00ff><!--purple-->
(Thanks to
Nigel Morrison,
arted@chembrit.co.uk
for pointing this situation out!)
</font>

<a name = "biophysj2002apr8"></a>
<!--
<a href =
"http://www.biophysj.org/cgi/content/full/82/3/1537?maxtoshow=&HITS=10&hits=10&RESULTFORMAT=&searchid=1018304730008_3318&stored_search=&FIRSTINDEX=0&volume=82&firstpage=1537&journalcode=biophys"

<table align = "right" border = 1>
<tr>
<td>
<a href =
"http://www.biophysj.org"
target = "lefthandedDNA"
><img src = "icons/biophysj2002apr8.gif" align = "right" border = 0
alt = "permission granted for image">
</a>
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>
&#169;
<a href =
"http://www.biophysj.org/misc/terms.shtml"
target = "lefthandedDNA"
>2002 by the Biophysical Society</a>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<a href =
"http://www.lecb.ncifcrf.gov/~toms/leftpolicy.html"
target = "lefthandedDNA"
><img src = "icons/permission.granted.gif" align = "right" border = 0
alt = "permission granted for image"></a>
<li>
<a href =
"http://www.biophysj.org"
target = "lefthandedDNA"
>Biophysical Journal</a>
published a paper,
<b>
   U. Bockelmann, Ph. Thomen, B. Essevaz-Roulet, V. Viasnoff, and F. Heslot,
   Unzipping Left Handed DNA with Optical Tweezers:
   High Sequence Sensitivity and Force Flips
<a href =
"http://www.biophysj.org/cgi/content/full/82/3/1537?maxtoshow=&HITS=10&hits=10&RESULTFORMAT=&searchid=1018304730008_3318&stored_search=&FIRSTINDEX=0&volume=82&firstpage=1537&journalcode=biophys"
target = "lefthandedDNA"
>Biophys. J. 2002 82: 1537-1553</a>
</b>
(the link requires that you have a subscription)
which is a cute demonstration of the power of modern technology.
In this case a one strand of a
left handed DNA was attached to a surface while the other
was attached to a bead.  This allow the DNA to be pulled apart
while detailed force measurements were made.
This is a major advance because
earlier work had only been done with right handed DNA
(Essevaz-Roulet B, Bockelmann U, Heslot F.
Mechanical separation of the complementary strands of DNA.
<a href =
"http://www.pnas.org/cgi/content/full/94/22/11935"
target = "lefthandedDNA"
>PNAS 1997 Oct 28:11935-40.</a> you <b>can</b> access this one)
<img src="icons/binoc.gif" alt = "binoculars" width = 15> First reported on 2002 April 8,
<img src="icons/new.gif" alt = "new"> as of 2002 April 8
<br clear = all>

<li>
<a href =
"http://www.amazon.com/exec/obidos/ASIN/081353030X/qid=1019231334/sr=1-1/ref=sr_1_1/102-7233022-5296106"
target = "lefthandedDNA"
>Digital Ratio</a>
by John T. Manning
(<a href =
"http://rutgerspress.rutgers.edu/"
target = "lefthandedDNA"
>Rutgers University Press</a>, ISBN:
<a href =
"http://165.230.98.36/acatalog/__Digit_Ratio_616.html#879"

>081353030X</a>,
February 1, 2002 )
apparently describes new digital methods of inserting left handed
DNA into the left hand.  Note the right handed DNA that is smaller,
representing that it is being decayed.

<a name = "genetics.wustl"></a>
<a href =
"http://www.genetics.wustl.edu/"
target = "lefthandedDNA"
><img src = "icons/genetics.wustl2002may8.gif"
align = "right" border = 0></a>
<a href =
"http://www.lecb.ncifcrf.gov/~toms/leftpolicy.html"
target = "lefthandedDNA"
><img src = "icons/permission.requested.gif" align = "right" border = 0
alt = "permission requested for image"></a>
<li>
In a recent biotechno-nanological advance,
all of the 
<a href =
"http://www.genetics.wustl.edu/"
target = "lefthandedDNA"
>model organisms</a>
at the
Dept. of Genetics,
Washington University School of Medicine 
("the fruit fly <i>Drosophila melanogaster </i>, the nematode worm
<i>Caenorhabditis elegans</i>, the baker's yeast <i>Saccharomyces
cerevisiae</i>,  the zebrafish <i>Danio rerio</i>, and the house mouse <i>Mus
musculus </i>"
and also the green algae <i>Chlamydomonas reinhardtii</i>
and the mysterious brown blob <i>unidentifiablitas probablycilliatii</i>)
were converted to left handed DNA.  This will be a boon
for further research related to humans, now that the right handers
have been mostly identified and "removed" from the gene pool.
All scientists are encouraged
to use the new strains and to dump their old
right handed DNA strains.
<img src="icons/binoc.gif" alt = "binoculars" width = 15> First reported on 2002 May 8,
<img src="icons/new.gif" alt = "new"> as of 2002 May 8.
The image on the web site was corrected within 2 days!
<img src="icons/thumbs.up.gif" alt = "thumbsup">
<img src="icons/thumbs.up.gif" alt = "thumbsup">
<img src="icons/thumbs.up.gif" alt = "thumbsup">
<img src="icons/thumbs.up.gif" alt = "thumbsup"> as of 2002 May 10
<font size = 3 color = #ff00ff>
(Thanks to
Lakshmanan K. Iyer,
laxman@hms.harvard.edu
for pointing this one out!)
</font>
